O sons, since I have fulfilled this book for you, which is the end of the science of medicine, by whose completion in it its end has come to be, and through whose expositions and declarations, it is apparent to me that you will complete that which concerns you with this treatise, which is a part of the operation by hand, so it is surgery.
Because the operation by hand is spread throughout our realm and in our time deprived of all, while the science of it greatly perishes, its visions are obscured, and nothing remains of it except a few small descriptions in the books of the ancients, which alter most things and lead to those errors and hesitations that are hidden within their minds, and the strength of the art is prolonged.
And it appears to me that you will enliven that through the arrangement of this treatise in accordance with the way of exposition and declaration and abbreviation; and so that the forms of the tools of cautery and other instruments of the work come through addition of the declaration, and through preparation of them, which cause is necessary.
But the cause for which no good artist is attained by his hand in our time is that the art of medicine is long, and that its performer must be exercised in the science of anatomy, which knows the parts of members and their forms, and the conjunction and separation of them; and has the knowledge of bones, nerves, and sinews, and the number of those, and their exit; and of the pulsating veins and the quiet ones, and the places of their issuing.
And because of that Hypocras says that by name is much, but in the work little, and especially in the art of the hand.
But we have already said that in the introduction of this book; for whoever is not learned in what we have said about anatomy is not evacuated so that he does not fall into error by which men will die, as I have seen many of those, for some are formed in this science and throw themselves from it without knowledge or experience.
For I saw a foolish and experienced doctor open an abscess of scrofula on the neck of a woman, and cut some arteries of the neck so that much blood flowed, and the woman died under his hands.
And I saw another doctor who, when he was called, pulled out a stone from a man who had a process in his state, and the stone was great, and he came to him and took out that stone with a piece of the bladder, and that man died on the third day.
And I called upon extracting that one, and I saw that because of the size of the stone and the disposition of the patient I knew he would die.
And I saw another doctor who took stipends from a duke of our land for surgery, and thus he did on a black slave whose leg was fractured; and prepared by his ignorance, extracted the fracture over the wound with powders and splinters with strong compression, and did not allow the wound breath.
And he bandaged it according to his desires, and he lay for several days and commanded that I loosen the bandage, and so he was for many days and commanded that I loosen the bandage; and he stayed like this until the thigh and leg were apostemated, and came to perdition.
And I was called to him, and I prepared to loosen the bandage, and there was tranquillity and relief from his pains.
But indeed the corruption was confirmed in the member, so that I could not restrain it and the corruption did not cease to wander in the member until the patient perished.
And I saw another doctor who perforated a cancerous abscess, and it was ulcerated for several days, until the malignancy of it grew great with its lord.
The which cause is that the old cautery is pure of melancholic humor, and it does not come to it from iron in any way, but that it be in a member who suffers so that it comes from all.
And because of that, sons, it is necessary for you that the operation by hand be devised in two ways: so is in operation to him who is associated with health, and in work to whom it is perilous in the greatest dispositions.
And I have already exhorted that in every place of this book where there is an operation in which error and fear come; so that it is necessary for you to guard that and leave that, so that the foolish do not find a way to speak and discourage you.
And thus take your weapons with solicitude and with protection of God, and your patients with facility and firmness.
And use the better way through the grace of God that they may go to health and glorious success, and lay those terrible diseases of difficult cure, and display your weapons from that which makes you fear, so that solicitude in your faith and in your name does not abandon you: for it remains to your glory, and beyond in the world to your sanctities.
Gaulish already said in some of her warnings: Do not heal wicked illnesses, so that wicked doctors are not named.
And I have devised this book according to three chapters.
Begins the division of the book, and first of the first part. The first chapter ordered from the head into feet, in the forms of the instruments and the tools of cauteries, and what is necessary in the operation.
The division of the second part. The second chapter, of bleeding and piercing, and of cupping, and of wounds, and of extraction of thorns and similar things, all capitoloized and ordered in the forms of the instruments.
The third chapter, of restoration and dislocation, and of cure of torture and of similar things; capitoloized from the head into feet, and of the forms of the instruments.
That I may tell you the quality of the elements of that cautery and of its pains, and in which complexions cautery is prohibited.
Then I say that the word of the quality of the element of cautery and of its pain is long and prolix, and subtle through science, and secret hidden; and many wise have spoken much about it, and they have been disagreed about it, and for me a little of their words is enough, fearing prolixity.
And thus I say that cautery confers universally on all complexions which are by matter or by its matter, excepting two complexions, which are cold complexions.
In the cold and dry complexions by matter, they are disagreed; for some say that cautery is beneficial to it, and others say the contrary of that, that cautery is not convenient in an illness which is by quality and dryness, for fire's nature is quality and dryness, and it is convenient that an illness be cured when cold and dry.
And he says the contrary of that, that cautery is done benefit in all illnesses cold and dry which we see in the bodies of men, for when you compare the body of man and its moisture to the complexions of fire, you rub the body of man dry.
And I say through his word that experiment that one has already seen me several times to be discovered.
However, we do not believe that anyone should come to this who is not experienced and practiced in the chapter of cauteries by recent exercises, and has known the diversity of complexions of men and the disposition of diseases in their bodies, and the causes of those, and their accidents, and the space of time of those.
But in other complexions, there is no fear over them, especially in cold and moist diseases; for all doctors have agreed on those, and are not disagreed on using cauteries on them.
And know, children, that the secret of healing by cautery with fire and of its goodness is that cauteries with medicine that burns are when fire is simple, whose action does not reach except to the member which is cauterized and does not affect another member adjusted to it except with a small injury.
But the action of medicine that burns reaches to those members from which it is distant; and it makes difficult healing come strongly in the member with disease, and strongly it drives it out.
But fire, through its sublimity and the goodness of its substance, does not do that except to make superfluous; And it is known to us by experience and by longevity of service of study on the truth of causes; And because of this I am excused from the length of speech. And if it were not that I was not read in my free time in this pause with you, a secret hidden with fire, and the quality of the operation of it in bodies and its expulsion by which diseases are expelled, with philosophical speech demonstrated, which would be acute in your ears.
And know, children, that they disagree on the time when it is convenient to do cauteries, and they pause the best time in spring.
And I say that cauteries are convenient in all times, because the harm by the accidental time is submerged in the benefit of what is drawn by cautery, and especially if the cautery is for pain or of necessary pains, strong and fiery, which do not suffer delay so that evil comes from them, which is greater than a little harm coming through time.
And therefore do not fear in your ears, o children, that which is not judged except by the common people and foolish doctors; It is that to that disease which is healed by cautery there is no reduction of time at all, and they pause on the consequence of it.
And the cause is not as they think it is, because cautery is only in place of medicine which alters the complexion and dries up the humidities which are the cause that pain comes.
But cauteries are superfluous over medicine by willfulness of their strength and power of their operation, and greatness of their Lord.
And it is possible that his disease will return at some time, according to the complexion of the patient and the location of his disease, and its power, and what is prepared in his body by the accumulation of superfluities in him, and what comes to him in his acquisition in foods and like those causes; But that disease in which cautery is administered is a subtle disease in a member with few superfluities and humidities, as well as cauterization of a tooth for pain and likeness to that, it is possible that the same pain will not return in him, but this happens little.
And popular speech is that cautery is the last remedy of medicine; And it is a true saying, not that they understand in it, because they think that there is no healing that benefits from medicine or anything else besides cautery.
And the cause is contrary to that; And the intention that cautery is the last remedy of medicine is only when we administer the methods of healing in some diseases, and those medicines do not confer it, and especially at the end of the cause we administer cautery to them, and it benefits that. And from here we see that cautery is not the last remedy of medicine according to the intention which the vulgar and foolish doctors understand it.
And the ancients said that cautery with gold is better than cautery with iron, and they did not say that except for the equality of gold and sublimity of its substance.
And they said that the place of cautery does not make brawn, although that is not absolutely true, because I have seen that it only happens in some bodies and not in others; And cauterization with that is good and better than with iron as they say; But when you do cauterize with fire with gold, it is not declared to you, because it is ignited according to the quantity that you want for the rust of gold, and because it prepares to receive friction; And if you adjust it on fire, some leaves are heated so that the gold-smith can pick them up with the occupation of that.
And because of this, cauterization with iron is done to us more lightly and closer to rectitude of operation.
And I have ordered this chapter on cautery according to chapters, and I have ordered those of the head into the feet, so that they ask me what they want from it.
The first chapter.
This cauterization confers with humidity and coldness on the servant accident of which there are two causes; It is a cause of soda and multitude of fluxes, called rhachitis, from the parts of the ears and the nostrils flowing, and multitude of sleeping, and pain in teeth, and of the golaryro, and universally of all disease that comes through friction, as well as paralysis, stroke, and like in those diseases.
The form of this cauterization is this: that first you command the patient to be evacuated by laxative medicine for three or four days, according to the strength of the patient and his age and habit.
And then command that he should shave his head with a razor, and then make him lie between your knees on a low bench, and let him rest his feet on your lap.
And then pause the edge of your palm above his nose between his eyes. But however, when the place of your middle finger is reached, sign that place with your thumb, and then ignite the cautery olive, of which this is the form:
And then pause that over the place marked with your thumb, in such a way that it descends, and while your hand is pressed little by little, you revolve that.
And then lift your hand lightly and look at the place, and if you see some quantity of the head removed, as well as the quantity of hair, then lift your hand again, and if it does not return with that iron, with another, if it is chilled, while that is seen by the eye what I have said to you.
And then take a little salt and dissolve it over the place and let him lick that for three days.
And then pause upon it the cloth soaked in vinegar and let it lie on it while the ash of the fire is destroyed. And then heal the place with tetrapharmacon unguent while it is healed.
And they said that when the Roman plaster is open, as many meals are poured onto the brawn, because it is better and more beneficial.
And they said that the tendon should be cauterized within the bone, and the cautery should be held within it while some part of the nature of the bone is burned.
And then it should be wiped with that which is astringent to the bone, and then healed.
Others say that with the cautery it reached the bone, as the strong pressure on the bone made the bone break, and this is the form of small aphecata. And they believe that from that location the vapor from the head expires.
And if the ulcer has been open for a long time, it should be treated until it is healed and consolidated.
But I have not seen these two types of cautery at all, except in a few cases out of fear; and for me, it is better that he be saved, if possible, because the head is devastated by the solution of continuity as we have testified in other members, especially when the patient's head is of a frail nature.
And the first type of cautery is safer and better for me, and I use it both ways to save lives.
Chapter two:
Of cauterization of the head:
When in all the head occurs a chronic and long-lasting pain and this is prolonged in the patient, and he uses garlic and leeches and scabies and plasters and poultices and bandages and hot stones and similar things, especially if he has already been cauterized with a cautery but none of those remedies benefit him, then consider that if the patient's head is of a strong creation in its nature, and not frail, and you find a great deal of coldness there, then cauterize it again with another cautery beneath that one a little. Then cauterize the top of his head with a cautery until the scalp is split open. The scalp split open and the quantity we have recounted discovered and cauterized with a cautery in the back of the head in the place which is called a vessel — and it is that place in which the ventouse is stopped. And press your hand there and do not discover the bone, because through this the patient feels great pain unlike all other cauterizations of the head.
And I say this cautery in its place, because the cautery with which you cauterize the horns of the head and the occiput is a more subtle cautery than that with which the middle of the head is cauterized; and this is the form of it:
Chapter three:
Of the cauterization of not antique emigration:
When a strong pain in the front of the head or a pain extending to the eye, the head should be evacuated with medicated cleansing agents, and the other cure should be administered, which I have described in the diseases of the head; and if all fails, cauterization is done in two ways: either cauterization with an acute burning medicine, or with iron.
But cauterization with a burning medicine is to take a piece of charcoal, and to scorch it and put the ends of it on both sides of the head. Then cut the site of the pain in the temple with a sharp-pointed lance until it is done underneath the scalp, so that it is hidden.
And then press on it with a descent constriction, and let it remain there for three hours; and then remove the charcoal and treat the wound with cotton soaked in wine or vinegar until the location turns purple.
And then heal that with ointment once it is healed.
And if you want to do this with some burning medicines which I have written about in the two treaties on burning medicines:
But the cauterization with iron according to this manner: heat the cautery of which this is the form:
and it is called a peg-like nail, because the head of it is like the form of a peg with some curvature, and in the middle of it is a small protuberance.
Then rest your hand on the site of the pain — so that the nail is not burned underneath — and rotate the iron little by little with speed.
And if the amount of it that is burned by the thickness of the scalp as far as the size of the nail, and raise your hand so that the artery underneath is not burned, so that no flow of blood comes.
Then moisten cotton in salt water and place that on the site, and let it remain for three days; and then place cotton moistened with wine, and heal with ointment once it is healed.
And if you want to cauterize with the tip of the scalpel according to cautery, and beware of cutting the artery in this not-ancient emigration:
Chapter four:
Of the cauterization of antique emigration:
When you cure emigration with what we have said about the preceding cure, and what we have said in the divisions of diseases, and the cure does not benefit, and you see that the disease which does not suffice it is what we have said about the first cautery with medicine or the cautery with fire, then where you ignite the cautery-scalpel until it turns white, then indicate on the site of the pain with a line the length of which is the size of your finger, or however much; and having pressed your hand there for an outlet and strengthened that there, pierce the artery within and enter into the bone.
However, it is necessary for you to guard against the continuance of the mandible, which is damaged by chewing, and that the joint not be burned and the nerve which controls that, and that no spasm ensue.
And if it is about the custody and care of the flow of blood from the artery which has burst, because there is danger in the burst of it, especially to him who ignores what he does and is not skilled and experienced; let the operation be done at the beginning.
And I will come with a reminder of the regimen for accidental blood flow from an artery according to its own manner in your free book.
And if you see that this disease does not suffice this cautery and the body of the patient tolerates it, cauterize in the middle of the head as we have recounted, and heal the wound once it is healed.
And if you want to administer the cautery which we have said in the chapter on stretching of an artery with cautery having two edges, it is better and more appropriate cauterization.
Chapter five:
Of the cauterization of the pains of ears.
When in the ear there is pain from cold and it is cured by lactic causes and with the other cure which we have said in the division, and the pain does not cease, then ignite the cautery, and this is the one which has the name puncta, of which this is the form:
Then pierce it with the point and let it be guided around all of the ear as it is rotated, or in the circuit of whichever one if the pain is in either one. Lengthen you with the cautery of the root of the ear little by little so that it tests the place with feeling.
And if the cautery of ten points in all the ear, and which one, then heal the sites once they are healed.
Chapter six:
Of the cauterization of torture:
Torture which is cured and cauterized with spices which come from phlegm, according to what we have said in the divisions of diseases.
Avoid cauterization with the species which comes from dryness or by spasm of the nerve.
When you cure that species of torture with herbs and poultices and compresses, and your cure does not confer, then where you cauterize the patient with three cauteries: the first pierces the root of the ear, the second a small one underneath the temple, and the third pierces the junction of two lips.
And apply your cautery to the opposite side of the sick person, because softness does not appear except on the side where there is an appearance of healing.
And the way of cauterizing is that you cauterize with the cautery on the right side of the upper part of the ear, and in the temple another, the length of which is according to the length of the finger toe, and place your hand holding the cautery until half the thickness of the hair has been burned.
And this is the form of the cautery, and it is a special instrument of the surgeon, the form of which is shown in front of the table. However, more subtle is that one a little like this; and cover what is in it of superfluous thickness a little.
Then treat the area with what is before it has been processed until it is healed.
Chapter .vii.
Of cauterization of ancient apoplexy.
When apoplexy is old and you treat it with what we have said, and your medication does not work, and the patient does not have a fever, then cauterize him with four cauteries: on all the horns of his head; and with cautery in the middle of his head, as we have said; and cauterize him in the neck. And the manner of cautery is done according to what has been said above.
And it has already been cauterized over the mouth of the stomach; and it is more behind; and let it be cured with what processes.
Chapter .viii.
Of cauterization of oblivion made by flame.
Let large jars and pills be given to the patient in the beginning by the servant who cleanses them.
Then scrape all of the patient's head and place on his neck a poultice of cedar described in the treatise on poultices.
And apply it several times, for that is the way of cautery. And do that according to what medicine orders which we have already said.
And if he heals well by those, and if not, then do to him three cauteries in the back part of his head, which descend from the highest part of his head into the lowest part of his neck. And place between each cautery the thickness of a finger, and treat the cautery with what has been said.
But if you want to increase it and the patient can tolerate it, then do cautery in the middle of his head; and if you see greater increase, then cauterize him on both horns.
Then treat that until it is healed; and let the cautery be olive-colored according to the form given before.
Chapter .ix.
Of cauterization of paralysis and of softening of all the body.
Let the servant cleanse his head by jars and what we have said; and scrape the patient's head, then cauterize him with cautery in the middle of his head, and with cautery on all the horns of his head, and with cautery on the back part of him, and with three cauteries on the vertebrae of his neck.
And if it is necessary in the softening of the body's paralysis that he tolerates and the illness is strong, then cauterize him with four cauteries on the vertebrae of his back, and finally with cautery until half the thickness of the hair has been burned, and raise your hand. Then treat that until it is healed, and let that be an olive-colored cautery.
Chapter .x.
Of cauterization of epilepsy.
The epileptic is not cauterized unless the epilepsy is due to phlegm.
And so let the servant cleanse his brain with herbs and with the other treatment which we have said in the division when the patient is large and tolerates the exception of medicines.
And if the patient is a child who does not endure medicines, then let the servant administer to him healing drinks and masticators, before meals, with good food.
Then scrape the patient's head, and then cauterize him with one cautery in the middle of his head according to the preceding manner, and with another cautery on the back of his head, and cauterize on each horn of his head. And if the patient is strong and tolerates it, then cauterize him with cauteries which I have said, having paralysis and softening of the body on the vertebrae of the neck and the back.
And let it be an olive-colored cautery according to what is preceded.
But if the patient is a child, then apply a subtle cautery according to this manner.
Chapter .xi.
Of cauterization of melancholy.
When the causes of melancholy are corrupted humidities and thick phlegm, then cauterize him with the cauteries which we have said in having paralysis.
And if the cause of melancholy is excess phlegm declining into melancholy, and the patient' body is cold, then let him drink what cleanses his brain according to what has preceded in the division.
Then scrape the patient's head, and then make a decently twisted bandage of it over the circle, and place that on the middle of his head.
And let the patient be in a sweat, and let him be held in every part.
Then apply a liniment; and it is more worthy of an old ram of sheep. And let that be heated over the fire with moderate heat, which he endures when it is within him. Then apply that in the middle of his head on the circle, and let him be within until he is deflated.
And do that to him once every week, with his other regimen well within until he is healed; or if you wish, cauterize him making small points according to his temperature, but have your hand on a cautery otherwise ordered: this is a special cautery which moistens the brain equally.
Then apply that cloth wrapped in wool or in flannel of wool.
Chapter .xii.
Of cauterization of water descending into the wound.
When you have declared the beginning of water in the wound by the signs which we have said in the division, then begin and give to drink what cleanses his head, and guard it from all humidities.
And let that boil in water in a pot for several days.
Then command him to wash his head, and cauterize him with cautery in the middle of his head; and cauterize him with two cauteries on two temples if there is the beginning of water in two wounds, or on one side if there is the beginning of water in a wound.
Enter with cautery all the veins and vessels which are beneath the skin; and let the cauteries be as long in length as the temples are wide.
Guard against blood flow when he is cauterized; and if you see from him blood flow, or stop it with some medicine, which is to you possible.
And come to know by experience which is in extraction of vessels and with incision of those, and guard against flow of blood.
And some vessels are cauterized beneath the bones at two cauteries produced by the end.
Chapter .xiii.
Of cauterization of ancient tears.
When tears from the eyes are chronic and flow through the veins and arteries that are in the obvious place on the head and it is confirmed that this is caused by excessive phlegmatic humors, then cauterize with a cautery the one which we mentioned in the chapter on water entering the eyes, and with a cautery in the middle of the head and with two cauteries below the temples, and with two cauteries below the bones. And if there is a need for more cautery, then cauterize all around the eye socket above the eyebrow with a small cautery.
Chapter 14
On cauterization for nose bleeds.
When you treat someone with what we have said in the division and your treatment does not help, then prepare and give the patient enemas for three nights; then shave his head and cauterize the middle of his head with a small cautery made from olive oil. Then cauterize the top of the nose with two cauteries above the eyebrows under the hair, a little one.
And be careful with the cautery so that you do not burn the area.
This is the form of the cautery made from cloves.
Chapter 15
On cauterization for softening the eyelids of the eyes.
When the eyelid of the eye is softened due to illness or humidity, then cauterize that eyelid with a cautery made from this lunar cautery; and this is its form:
And if you, cauterize that above the two eyebrows a little with two cauteries all around, and let them be extended from the temples. And let the length of each cautery be according to the size of the eyebrow.
And do not press too hard with your hand on the cautery, but according to the amount that the upper part of the eyelid is adhered.
Chapter 16
On cauterization for the eyelid of the eye when hairs turn to the inside and poke the eye.
When the hairs of the eye turn inside and poke the eye, there are two ways to treat it: either with a cautery made from fire or with a burning medicine.
If you treat it with fire cautery, order the patient to open his eyes so that you may cut off the hairs at their roots, while they are prolonged and curling; and if he pokes his eye, then you should compress his eye with a bandage so that it is not damaged until the hairs have grown back.
Then when they have grown back and curling, then place the patient's head in your lap, and apply a sign with ash on the eyelid of his eye with a sign, the shape of which is like that of a dead leaf; let the beginning of this sign be close to the hairs.
Then place cotton soaked in white vinegar or in mullein tea on the eye.
And heat the cautery, of which this is the form:
Then cauterize over the figure which you have marked, little by little, in small spots, until the surface of the eyelid which is like that of a myrtle leaf is completely burnt and appears properly.
And a sign of the health of your work is that you see the eyelid of the eye already contracted and the hairs of it elevated above the puncture of the eye.
And so elevate your hand and lay that for three days, and then place on it a poultice with a bandage until the scar is healed. And then treat that with ointment until it is healed.
But if the hairs return for any cause and the eyelid is softened again, then do the cautery over that area again as you did first.
But if the hairs are under the eyelid, then cauterize that area so that it is elevated to its natural place and is curling and the hairs do not poke the eye.
But the cautery with burning medicine is that you order the patient to open his eyes so that the hairs are prolonged and curling; and then let it be made of linen the shape of a myrtle leaf.
And then take of soap with aconite one ounce. [ missing text ] Take that and mix it with honey, and then make that into a poultice so that it is not too loose; and apply that on the linen which is made like a myrtle leaf, and place that on one eye or both eyes; and let the patient's head be in your lap.
And hold the end over the medicine and press that a little and do not harm him until the patient feels the biting of the medicine, for it bites like fire.
And when you perceive that it bites, then take away the medicine and moisten that with your finger; therefore when the biting ceases, then remove the medicine and wash the eye with water; and then consider if you see the eyelid already lifted as it is washed by fire or by incision, for then it is well; and if it returns to that of the medicine on the area where the medicine has not bitten and does not turn black until your operation is healed and the eye is contracted.
And then place on it a poultice with a bandage until the scar of the abrasion is healed, and then treat that with ointment made from defininicum or another one until it is healed.
And it is necessary for you that after your operation to be careful with great diligence that in the eye does not fall any of the medicine; and if the eyelid after a few days becomes softened and for some reason the hairs poke the eye, then repeat your medicine over that area which of the eyelid became softened and just as you did at the beginning, and compress it until it is healed.
And this is the shape of the myrtle leaf.
And know that the eyes of men are diversified according to their magnitude and smallness, therefore according to that is made your contraction.
And do not moisten the end of the wand in water for that person to whom it is taught in this art.
Chapter 17
On cauterization for a fistula that is in the tear duct of the eye.
When you treat a fistula with what we have said in the divisions of illnesses and your treatment does not help, then do that you cauterize that according to this way: order the patient to place his head in your lap, and let him hold his head by someone between his hands in such a way that it is not harmed or moved with his head.
Then place on the eye of him cotton soaked in white vinegar or in mullein tea.
And heat the cautery, of which this is its form:
which shall be hollow, like a straw or a feather, from one end of which the cautery is made.
And if you see that it penetrates into the other end. And if you want to be drawn out like a tent.
But what is hollow is better for your work.
And then press the fistula if it is open, and draw from it pus and dry that. And if it is not open, then open it and extract the swelling of it.
And then place on it the cautery that is well kindled, and hold your hand over the eye to prevent the patient from moving or the patient being disturbed, and then place the cautery on the grays of the eye and touch that.
And if at the beginning of your cautery you perceive that it has entered into the bone, that is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery one or two times, if necessary; and leave it like that for three days, and then place over that spot a poultice and surround it with a dried ointment until it is healed.
But if eleven days have passed and it has not healed, then place over it a sharp, corrosive medicine until the bone is uncovered and is scraped according to what is convenient in its chapter.
And there is another way to do cauterization of a fistula, which they first said: enter into the location of the fistula and cover it; and then pause in that fissure a subtle caustic of which its form is made of. And pour into that the quantity of one ounce of molten lead; and hold your hand with the caustic with good retention, and do not harm the patient so that the molten lead does not reach the hole of that; and cover what you pause over the hole of the patient with a cloth soaked in vinegar or water, for the lead will adhere to the location of the fistula and heal it through marvelous healing.

Chapter 46.
Of cauterization of dislocation.
When it happens in any limb due to displacement or impact and the pain persists for a long time and does not yield to any healing with medicines, then you should cauterize with a pointed cautery.
And the cautery of every limb should be according to its size and smallness, nature and dislocation of it, location and pain of it.
So if it is healed by the first cautery, then it is good; and if not, then repeat the cautery on it; for it is customary of those pains to be punctured from one limb to another nearby, so that you achieve it with the cautery until the patient is healed.
Chapter 47.
Of cauterization of leprosy.
The lepers in some ways are cauterized for great benefit, and greatly if the nature of leprosy is made by putrefaction of fire and melancholy.
And so when you want to cauterize that person, consider if the leprosy is composite and not cured by what we have said in the division or vice versa, and beware of the patient if the corruption extends throughout his body and all its functions, then cauterize him on the head with five cauteries: one in the middle part of the head, and the second below it, avoiding the forehead; and both over the horns; and one behind, over the nape or neck.
And finally, place your hand with the cautery a little onto the bone so that it makes a slight impression, and of him there are displaced the scars so that the expiration of thick vapors is alleviated from him; and cauterize on the pustules according to what precedes.
And if the leprosy is already expanded over the body and appears manifestly, then you should cauterize him with those cauteries which we have mentioned in the head; and cauterize on the end of the nose, and two cauteries on the two jawbones, and two cauteries on the two collarbones, and six cauteries on the two shoulders, and one large one on the collarbone near the chest, and one on the breastbone, and two on the hips, and two on each thigh, and two on each leg, and one on each ankle, and two on the heels.
And beware of the nerves which are over the heel so that you do not burn those.
And sometimes it is cauterized with a cautery on the arm's bone and another on the mouth of the stomach, and another on the anus.
And be aware that when you draw closer the cauteries, there is greater benefit and they work better; and be aware that the patient does not have pain from the cautery, as well as those who have it, for the body of him is already stunned.
And see that the cauteries are large or small according to the limbs and joints according to which it is preceded by the properties of the tools.
And then treat the cautery wound with a paste made of rye flour, honey, and other healing remedies until it is healed.
Chapter 48.
On cauterization for paralysis.
When a limb is paralyzed or asleep and is not healed by medicines, oils, and poultices, then cauterize the affected limb with a cautery according to the size of the limb or its smallness.
And let your cautery enter the thickness of the vein a little. Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
And sometimes paralysis is cauterized where the pain travels to the hands and feet, and to the vertebrae of the back, and follows the path of the nerves which move that limb, and the paralysis is relieved.
But do not presume to do this unless you are skilled in the anatomy of the limbs and the exit of the nerves throughout the body.
Chapter 49.
On cauterization for white leprosy.
When leprosy is old and does not yield to any of the healing ointments, then cauterize it with a hot cautery a little according to the thickness of the vein until the whiteness and color are relieved, and its color changed.
Then treat it with a paste made of rye and lentil flour, roasted olive oil, and leaves of wild rosemary, and pigeon's blood or chicken's blood; let each ingredient be a portion, and all be mixed together; then dip a cloth in it and place it on the affected area until it is healed.
Chapter 50.
On cauterization for cancer.
When a cancer is starting and you want it to stop, then cauterize it with a circular cautery along its rim as it is being rolled.
And the wise say it should be cauterized with the last cautery in its very center; but I have not seen this, for I am afraid it might become ulcerated, and I have indeed seen this happen several times.
And it is more proper that it be cauterized along its rim with a circular cautery as we have said, or with pointed cauteries.
Chapter 51.
On cauterization for ulcers.
When an ulcer is slow to heal, and this is due to an excess of humor which causes it, or to the state of the patient, whether he is old and feeble, or lacking in blood, or due to the season, and you want to hasten the healing of the ulcer; then cauterize along its rim with small pointed cauteries; and then let it be bound up so that it will hasten in its healing.
But if you want to pierce it with a cautery, then heat the cautery of which this is the form: and place it on the very center of the ulcer so that it may penetrate the vein.
And if the cautery is in a place which is lower down, so that the arm may be stretched out; then treat it until it is healed.
Chapter 52.
On cauterization for sores.
A sore is a corruption that wanders in the limb, which corrodes it as fire consumes dry wood.
Therefore, if you see this sore in a place which can endure cauterization by fire, then heat small nail-shaped cauteries, and large ones according to what is appropriate in the place where the sore is.
Then cauterize it all over so that you remove all the corruption and do not leave any cause for it to return.
Then let the patient lie for three days, and place a poultice of ground herbs soaked in mixed oil over the cauterized spots so that the scars are entirely eradicated and all corruption.
Then treat it with ointments that generate flesh.
But if after three days the flesh is born and is healthy where there is no corruption, that is good; but if not, then repeat the cauterization over what is rotten in the affected spots.
And sores are sometimes treated with sharp medicine, for that is mild in place of cauterization. But cauterization by fire is the surest remedy.
And I have already described its cure with sharp medicine in division, so take that one when you see an inclination towards cauterization.
Chapter 53.
On cauterization for warts and similar growths.
Many times there are growths beneath the sole of the foot which cause great pain, and they are called warts; and cauterization for these is according to two methods: by fire or by water.
But cauterization by fire is that you heat the cautery which is bent like the beak of an owl, and let it be made of iron according to the thickness, and let it have sharp edges.
Then place this heated cautery on the wart, and then revolve your hand with the cautery along the rim of the wart so that the cautery may reach the depth of the wart. Let him lie for three days until you see the arm begin to swell. Then treat it with a poultice made of crushed mallows and salt, and place the poultice on him for one night, for this will uproot it from its roots.
Then treat the spot with an ointment that generates flesh until it is healed.
And if it does not convert and sometimes occurs in the surface of the body, especially in the hands and feet, then understand that you must administer cauterization by water, and take a cannula made of horn or iron or the beak of an owl, and place this on the wart; and let him hold his hand tightly so that he does not move it, and pour a little water into the cannula and drop it on the root of the wart; and wait for the patient for one hour, then remove it, for the wart will have been uprooted with its roots.
And thus do for all of them wherever they occur in the body.
Then treat these warts whose roots are eradicated with ointments that generate flesh.
Chapter 54.
On cauterization for trembling.
When one suffers trembling from chafing of a nerve or from fever caused by chafing, or for some other cause similar to these, then understand that you must cauterize him with four cauteries or with V-shaped cauteries on the vertebrae of his back, between each vertebra with a cautery; and let him hold his hand tightly so that he does not move it, and cauterize the feet, and over the stomach with an olive-shaped cautery.
And the trembling of the patient subsides and the health of the chafed improves.
Chapter 55.
On cauterization for accidental wounds in the body.
When the body is pierced and pustules are formed which are opened by thick corrupt humors, then understand that it must be cauterized at the very beginning when it appears, and let it be cauterized over the entire orifice of the wound with a fine-pointed cautery made of myrtle wood, the end of which is extended by fire; or with the edge of a long pair of tweezers; or with a broad cautery.
And sometimes it is cauterized by the release of the humors at the beginning in this way; and if they are not broken, the corrupt humor is expelled causing them to be healed.
But nevertheless it is better that this be done with an evacuation through phlebotomy.

Chapter .VII.
Of the cure of inflammation of orifices.
Some children are born from the bellies of their mothers not perforated, and this happens in some men, inflammation in the middle of the orifice by plague or by flesh eaten into that; and this inflammation sometimes is done in the part of the orifice which does not reach the chest, and sometimes it is in the outer parts.
And what happens in the middle of it which does not reach the chest is in the major dispositions of difficult cure.
And in that in which you obtain the chest, be sure that you place the orifice of the patient towards the sun and observe it. And therefore if you see swelling appear, open it with a thin spathe of which this is the shape of it:
If the end of it in which there is a small width, in which one part is acute and the other is not, and the spathe is of two lion’s teeth on either side that it does not hurt the orifice.
And if the inflammation is by flesh born in the orifice or in the middle of it, then open that with fine tongs and cut that violently easily until all the flesh is removed.
And if the inflammation is in the middle of the orifice, then take a slender reed and heat it with fire a little, and then place that in the middle of the orifice.
And therefore if you have sensed swelling by that the patient feels with facility due to difficulty in breathing, relieve the oppression of it with facility; and be careful not to plague the nerves of the orifice.
And then place in the orifice cloth of linen according to the amount of the width of the orifice which you have wrapped in Egyptian ointment; and do this for several days until you are sure of the healing of the plague, or take some other and soak that in waters and powder over it vinegar mixed with triturated vinegar and use it; and if you see the orifice already pustulated, then be sure to pull off that which you wrapped in linen and replace it with another which you wrap in cerot made from refined oil until the pustule is cauterized; and then cure that until it is healed.
But if bleeding occurs, then soak sponge or cloth in warm water and place it over the orifice, and use the other cure which tightens the flow of blood, which is said in the tract of distillation.

And then after that is done, that he breathe his odorant into the passages of the nose, and let it sit on his gullet, for troops of sight make children in schools, and it is a light cause to him who wants to do so.
And then place the two extremities of the gall, one end that hangs over the nose and the other that pierces into the nose, and then administer meat with the nuts that are in the gall, and do this until you know that the meats already are on the nuts of the gall.
And then pull out the gall, and place in the nose properly an abstraction of blood a lump, and do this for three days or more until the meat has run off from the meats.
And then at the end of the cause, place in the nose a cannula of lead for several days until it is healed.
But if you see that there is no healing from the cure, use that.
And this is the form of the instrument to cleanse with which are distilled oils and medicines in the nose:

Chapter .XXV.
Of the thills in the extremities of the nose.
Many times there is pain in the extremities of the nose, and it is magnified and swollen for several days until it is taken off the guard of him.
And then you should insert that in the beginning of his appearance, and you remove all that. And then place over the place a cautery or burning medicine which is in the location of him.

And if the pustule is of meat touch and not of dusky color, and it is possible that abscission in all that, then evacuate the patient and insert that senseless, and bind the location with what bleeds and tightens so that it is healed.
Chapter .XXVI.
Of the sewing of the nose and of the lips and of the auricle when the continuity is dissolved by water or similarly.
You know that when there occurs solution of continuity in one of these cartilage, few confer in that operation but in some men.
Thus, when for any reason at all you see a fresh wound with its blood, then you stitch together the two lips of the wound and then heal that part which is inside.
And if the lips of a wound that is already separated are both healed, then you must open up each part of its flesh so that blood can come out. Then stitch together the two lips with a thread and strengthen them, then powder the wound with dragon's blood and cut up herbs, and place on this powder an ointment called diafinicon or another of the consolidating ointments, and let it remain there for two or three days, and then remove this ointment and change the ointment and leave it there until the wounds are healed by themselves, and then heal them with an ointment.
But the way of stitching is to join together the cut or with a needle as we have recommended in the stitching of the belly, or join the cut with fat just as you and your father have done and make it known.
Chapter 27.
Of the extraction of those causes that come from both lips in the parts within.
Many times we see in many men in the parts within their lips some hard growths similar to grainy err-like growths, and some of them are smaller while others are larger.
Thus you should turn the lips inside out and cut each of those hard growths from every part, and then stop the bleeding by filling the wound with cut sage, and wash it with wine and heal the place with something astringent until it is healed.
Chapter 28.
Of incision of the flesh attached to the gums.
Many times there is on the gum a growth of flesh which the ancients called albulus, therefore you should suspend it with tweezers or forceps and cut off that growth at its root, and let the pus run out or the blood flow.
And then place on the wound cut sage or other astringent or drying powders.
And if that flesh returns, then cut it off again and cauterize it, for by cauterizing it will not return.

And so if this is done at the beginning of the cause, that is good; and if not, then you should repeat the cause over them on another day and on the second and third until it comes to the final state that you want.

Chapter 30.
Of extraction of teeth.
You who should cure the tooth of its pain with every effort and delay in total extraction of it, for there is no cause that exceeds its appearance when it is extracted, for it is a sublime substance that is an obstacle to extraction.
But when the patient is forced to have that tooth extracted, then you must be certain which is the aching tooth, for often the pain deceives the patient and seems to be in a healthy tooth, and you extract that one and the pain does not cease until the aching and ill tooth is extracted, for we have seen this by the operation of barbers.
So when you are certain which is the aching tooth, and you know it well, then you should scarify all around the tooth with a spatula in which there is some medicine, so that it is dissolved from the gum all around, and then pull it with your fingers or with thin forceps little by little until you loosen it.
And then hold it firmly with strong fingers or a strong grip, and place the head of the patient between your knees so that he does not move his head, and then pull the tooth according to the straightest way without breaking it, nor giving great pain to the patient.
And so if it is not loose, then take one of those instruments and put it under it from every side with ease and force the removal of it just as it was done at the beginning.
But if the tooth is split or hollowed out, then you should fill that crack with cloth and tighten it with the tip of a subtle needle so that it is not cracked when you constrict it with your fingers.
And you should open completely with scarification all around the gum and guard your strength so that you do not crack it or tear a part of it, and return to the patient because of the malice so that his first pain is minor.
And take care that you do not do what some ignorant barbers do in their foolishness and audacity in extraction of them, for they do not administer what we have recommended; for often they bring upon men great diseases which are difficult to cure because they crack them or pull out bones from the jaw just as we have often testified.
And then the extraction of it should be washed with wine or wine vinegar and salt.
And if then it bleeds through that, then take some quantity of pyrite and spread it over the wound, and if it does not agree with that pyrite, cauterize.
The form of the pliers and tweezers with which you have cut the tooth at the beginning should be of long length, short handle, and thick, so that they are not bent when you operate with them.
And they should be as thick as a finger's length so that when you tighten them they are not bent, and they should have short handles.
And they should be made of Indian iron wrought with hammer and chisel, and in the tips of them there are teeth which some enter into others and tighten with great constriction, and sometimes the tips of those are shaped like the beak of an owl which is hooked, and it is strong.

Chapter .xxxii.
Of the curing of teeth that protrude above the others.
When teeth are prominent not according to their natural form, especially when it happens in women and especially in elderly women, then you should consider: if the tooth is next to another tooth and it is not possible for there to be a hole or groove made in it, then extract that tooth; but if it is attached to another tooth, then insert that with this instrument whose shape is as follows; it is similar to a pig's jaw, and it should be made of Indian iron with an acute end.
And your incision should be with which you insert that into it for several days because of the hardness of the tooth, and that it does not affect any of the other teeth.
And if there is a prominent tooth that has been sharpened, then file it with an Indian-iron file whose shape is as follows:
And it should all be made of Indian iron and the edge should be even; and it should have very fine points like a file with which needles are made, with which the tooth is filed little by little over several days with ease, so that no other tooth is affected or breaks. Then smooth it in the end, and scrape away at that with a razor-sharp knife.
And if there is a tooth of which one part is broken and it hurts the tongue, then you should smooth that until the sharpness of that fracture is blunted and smoothed out, so that the tongue is not hurt or speech impaired.
Chapter .xxxiii.
Of the restoration of broken teeth with gold or silver.
When it happens to teeth that they receive concussion and removal due to impact or being knocked out, and the patient is unable to hold down food, and your cure with astringent medicines has not helped, then so that they do not become rotten there should be made in those that are strengthened with gold and not silver; for silver becomes black and then rots after a few days, but gold never changes color.
And the gold should be in such thinness and thickness according to the amount that can enter between the teeth; and the shape of the filling is that you take the gold and place twice as much between two healthy teeth, and then that it grows with two curves of the gold between two broken ones, or if one or several, so that the fabric comes to the healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the filling into the part from which you begin, and strengthen your work with ease and skill so that it is not broken.
And your filling should be so that you strengthen the gold over the roots of the teeth so that it does not leak out.
And then cut off the excess gold with tongs or tweezers, and add those to each location with a gesture, and smooth them over and conceal them between the healthy tooth and the broken one, so that they do not hurt the tongue. And then let it be sealed as tight as possible with Roman pitch.
But if it is loose or broken, strengthen that or them with other gold in the same manner at all times.
And this is the form of teeth and the figure of the filling of two healthy teeth and two broken ones, as they are bound together:
And when a tooth or teeth return to their place after being knocked out and are grown back as we have described, and they become broken again.
And it does not happen unless by a wise and skilled artist.
And some have become apt for some cause of cow bones, and it is made into the shape of a tooth, and it is placed in the location where the tooth failed, and it is strengthened as we have said, and remains and serves with that person for a long time.
Chapter .xxxiv.
Of the incision of the ligament which appears on the tongue and prohibits speech.
This ligament which appears beneath the tongue in some people is either natural or happens accidentally due to a wound and has already healed.
And the operation on it is that you open the mouth of the patient, you see his jawbone beneath; and lift up his tongue, and then insert that nervous ligament according to its width so that the tongue is released from its binding.
But if there is any hardness or knotting in it, and that is standing due to the healing of a wound, then you pierce it with a needle and cut it according to its width so that you have cut the ligament and the knotting is dissolved.
And be careful that there is not made a crack in the membrane of the flesh and that you split the artery and blood flows out.
Then wash the traces of the incision with rosewater or with wine vinegar or with pure water; then place the tongue in its socket, so that it is not moved from its socket again until it has healed properly.
But if blood flows out, place vitriol on the spot; but if the blood oozes out, then cauterize the spot with a caustic that is appropriate for it. Then that is cured with the other causes so that it is healed.
Chapter .xxxv.
Of the extraction of a ranula beneath the tongue.
Sometimes it happens under the tongue an apostema resembling a wart that prevents the tongue from its natural movement, and when it becomes enlarged so that it fills the mouth.
And the operation on him is that you open the mouth of the patient facing the sun and consider the abscess.
And if you see it dark in color or black, hard, and the patient feels no pain in it, then do not touch that place.
And if it turns white, with moisture in it, then insert the needle into it and pour in some subtle vinegar; and if blood comes out during your operation, then place a wound pack over it that is made of vitriol, until the bleeding stops.
Then return to your work on it until it is healed with pleasant wine and salt; and cure it with another cure until it is healed.
Chapter 36.
Of the extraction of an abscess from two ears and that which is in the throat, and of other abscesses.
When an abscess occurs in the throat, there are glands which are clustered together like cotton; these are driven out and are named two ears; when you cure them with what we have said in the division, and they are not healed, then consider if the abscess is hard, dark in color, of little feeling, then do not touch that with iron.
And if it is of red color and the root is thick, then do not touch that with iron for fear of the flow of blood; before you leave it, let it become mature, and then either you will cut it out or it will burst by itself.
And if it is of white color, soft, and the root of it is subtle, then that is what should be pierced.
And the operation on him is that you consider, before your operation, if the abscess has rested on him completely or has diminished by any diminution; and then make the patient face the sun, his head in your lap, and open his mouth, and the servant holding his hands take hold of his tongue with a noble instrument of which this is the form:

So when you press with that the tongue and it is shown to you the abscess, and you put over that your vision, then take needle and pierce that in the hollows outward as far as you can.
