And all that knowing that it should be in the science of the midwife how strong she is to that small childlike thing, and therefore pause her at that with what she covers.
Chapter .lxxvi.
On extraction of the fetus when it is dead.
When you treat the dead fetus with what we have said in the division of medicine and it is not extracted, then know that you must use cauterization with fire, then consider if the woman is of sound nature and does not have any of the accidents by which there is feared loss over her.
Then know that the woman should be laid on her side on her back, and her head inclined downward and her thighs elevated.
And the minister should hold that of two parts or be laid on the bed so that the body of the patient does not extend due to the extraction of the fetus.
And then you moisten the mouth of the mother with oiled waters and with mucilage of alder and of fenugreek and of linseed.
And then the midwife holds her hand with those oils and mucilages, and then inserts her hand with ease in the mother, and then close with her hand the place where the nails are in the fetus.
And convenient places in that: then you consider if the fetus is prepared to descend over his head, and then you put the nails in the hollows of him, or in the neck, or in the mouth, or on the palate, or below the beard, or in the armpit, or in close places of the ribs, or below the hips.
But if the descent is over the feet of him, then in the instep, or in the middle of the ribs.
And know that he has the nail with his right hand, and pause the bending of him in that place which is between the fingers of his left hand, and insert your hand with ease and put the nail in some of those places which we have said, so that it arrives at the desired cause.
And then pause and put in the opposition of him another nail, or the third if it is necessary, so that there is equal attraction, and not extension according to its straightness only.
And then it is closed at all parts, so that the exit of him is drawn back as it is done in tooth extraction.
And know in him that the extension is softened; and if any part of him is retained, then know that the midwife anoint some of his fingers with oil and insert that wisdom and turn with those what is retained.
Therefore if the part of the fetus is forgotten, then the nails are moved into place where it is pushed out; and do that so that all the fetus is forgotten.
But if the hand is extracted in front of the other, and it is not possible to reduce that by the contraction of him, then know that cloth be wrapped over that; and then extract that so that all is forgotten, and insert that or in the joint of the hand or in the elbow.
And know that you do likewise in the other hand and in both feet.
But if the head of the fetus is large, or happens to him contraction in the exit, or there is water in his head tightly closed, then know that you insert between the fingers aspium spinosum and split the head and extract the water, or cut that with an instrument which is named "alundat", the shape of which comes with the shapes of other instruments in the chapter which is appropriate to this.
And do likewise if the fetus is of large head by nature: and then know that insert that with alum, or take that with forceps as we have said; and then extract the bones with the forceps.
If the head is drawn back and pressed against the collarbone, then the moisture in the feet should be released.
But if it is not complicated and there is no obstruction, then you should turn the head immediately as soon as it is possible for you.
And if the bottom of the abdomen is swollen or fluid-filled, then pierce it so that the moisture inside can flow out.
But if the gout is in your feet, then the attraction of it is weaker and its direction towards the mouth of the mother is weaker.
But if it is pressed against the abdomen and the feet, then cover that area with a cloth that you have wrapped around your hand, and pierce the abdomen and the feet so that what is inside them can flow out.
But if the other members are cut off and the head is turned, then the midwife should insert her left hand; but if the mouth of the mother is open, then insert the hand into the air sac, and surround that area with her hand, and pull it towards the mouth of the mother using those fingers, and then insert a thumb or two thumbs into it and move it around with them.
And if the mouth of the mother is blocked by a swelling that occurs due to an ulcer, then it should not be pressed on; instead, administer the application of moist and fatty causes. And apply poultices; and follow the patient with waters that soften and moisten.
And if it is on their side, then if possible, let it be tied down; and what we have said about the live foot should be administered.
And if that is not possible, then let it be cut into pieces, and extracted.
And so that no cause remains within the second part.
And when you have completed all your operations, then administer what we have said about the treatment of ulcers that arise in the mother.
And if bleeding occurs, then apply styptic waters and administer the other treatment.
And I have seen a woman in whose abdomen there was a dead fetus, and on that fire another pregnant one was placed, and the dead fire was the other fetus. It happened to her after a long time that there was an ulcer in her abdomen; and it inflated so that the fire opened up, and it began to spit out flames.
And I was called upon to heal it, and I treated it for a long time, but the ulcer did not heal.
Then I placed some poultices with strong attraction on it, and extracted a bone from it; and after a few days, I extracted another bone; and I was very astonished that there were bones in the abdomen.
And then I thought they were the bones of the dead fetus.
Then I opened up the ulcer and extracted many bones from her, and the woman remained in the same condition for a long time, recovering a little from the vomiting of flames.
And I did not think that this was strange, but rather that there is knowledge and aid in it for what the doctor does with his hand for healing.
Of the forms of instruments that are necessary to extract the fetus.
Form of opening with which the mother is opened:
This is the form of a strainer in which the lips are curved, and to that curvature there are two virtues at both ends of the two edges. But this virtue should be made more subtle with a tight-fitting, and it should be made of ivory or wood.
And the thickness of each stalk is as wide as two fingers and the rim of each is as wide as one finger, and the length of each is about a hand and a half; and in the middle part of the two stalks there are two adjustment holes of those stalks which have been fastened, and their length is about a palm and a little more, and the thickness of each is as wide as two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mother's mouth so that it is opened by them when you rotate those two prescribed virtues.
Other form, resembling that one but more subtle and longer:
It is made of ivory or wood according to the resemblance of forceps. But at the end of it there are two additions like a comb, and the length of those additions is about a hand, and the thickness of each is two fingers.
And when you want to open the mother with them, then have the woman lie on her back with her legs hanging down, opening what is between her thighs. And then open your hand with the instrument just as you do with tweezers according to the amount that you want of the opening of the mother's mouth, in such a way that the midwife does what you want her to do.
The form of a strainer which the ancients described:
Form of a lever with which the fetus is pushed:
Form of a screw with which the large head is crushed:
And it is like forceps with which the teeth are placed in the end, just as you do with forceps according to this form; and some times there is a long one made just like forceps, just as you do with forceps, whose teeth are placed just like the teeth of pliers. Form of a screw with few turns:
Form of an awl having one point:
Form of an awl having two points:
Form of a chisel having two edges with which the head is split:
Form of two broad chisels:
All those instruments are exercised, and when they are more polished and prepared by the artisans, then they are easier to operate and better for men because of their quantity.
And you should not be without those instruments, for the need for them is inexcusable.
Of extraction of the afterbirth:
When the afterbirth is retained after childbirth, then command the woman to retain her urine, and then make her vomit with cold water, and if it is excreted in this way, it is good.
And if not, then take a towel and pierce the covering with a hole, and place on it herbs that open the mouth of the mother, just as you do with pepper, and rue, and anise, and camomile, and aromatic plants, and cassia wood, and saintwood. All these herbs or some of them are to be immersed in water in the towel, and place that on the fire.
And then place a funnel of reed in the mother's mouth, and hold it so that the vapor comes to the cavity of the mother.
And then make her vomit just as we have said, and the afterbirth will come out forced with velocity.
But if this treatment is applied and it has not been effective, then command the midwife to immerse her left hand in sesame oil or in mustard water, and then place it in the birth canal and feel for the afterbirth, and when you have found it, extract it little by little according to the degree that the mother does not suffer a violent attraction. Before anything else, make sure the patient is moved to their right and left sides with ease.
And if the mother's mouth is constricted, we have already recommended a cure with snuff and medication of the gum with herbs.
But if it is not cured by all the causes we have recommended, then beware that you do not tire yourself out with iteration of abstraction, but rather, whatever comes out of him to the mother' mouth, place it in the woman's lap; and then christen it with ointment and tetrapharmacon. For this will cause it to rot in a few days, and be dissolved and cured.
But when it rots, there is bad odour that rises to the head and stomach, and this hurts the patient; and then one must administer sub-fuming and convenient remedies for this.
But some of the Ancients have been experienced in sub-fuming with nithy-tort and dry figs.
Form of instrument by which the woman is sub-fumed, for the retention of menses and secondina:
It must be made of brass or bronze.
And its subtle end is placed in the vulva, and its wide end over the fire; and the sub-fuming is placed over the embers of the fire.
And have another one ready that is consumed by that sub-fuming, and iterate another.
Of cure of the child' anus not perforated:
Some infants are born and their anuses are not perforated; thus, one should gently open them up and lubricate them. And then, if the midwife perforates it with her finger, that is fine; but if not, perforate it with a sharp spatulum, and beware of touching the testicles.
And then place wool soaked in wine and oil over it; and cure that with ointment until it is healed.
But if you fear that it is obstructed, then place a cannula in there for a few days, and remove it when the child wants to defecate.
And it sometimes happens that the anus is obstructed by a clot or by pus; then one must split that clot and cure it as we have said before about the cure and the plomb.
Of cure of fistulas that occur from there below:
The fistulas that occur below in the lower part are a swollen and thick node, which occurs near the anus or in the space between one part.
And it is a fistula, or several of them.
And when that node becomes old and runs with the white or yellow pus, then sometimes these fistulas penetrate.
And those that penetrate are known by what comes out of them through the exit and through flatulence; and sometimes it uses the patient' exit, and often it is expelled from there as worms.
And sometimes these fistulas penetrate into the bladder, and into the urethra; and sometimes they penetrate in between the junction of the hip and into the navel.
And what is known as a penetrating fistula that penetrates into the anus and does not penetrate, is to put your finger in there, and insert a thin probe of brass or iron into the fistula when it is not painful.
But if it is painful, then insert a subtle probe of lead or horsetail into there while you feel the probe or the hair in your finger.
And if you do not feel anything at all and there is no sign of defecation or worms or flatulence, then know that it is not penetrating; then go to the cure.
And when the fistula is penetrating into the bladder or urethra, then its meaning is the excretion of urine from there and prohibition of it so that medicine does not consolidate there.
But if it is penetrating into the hip joint or into the bone and there is pain in the hip, then its sign is that the probe goes in there if in the bone it is not painful, and there is pain in the circuit of the part; and from there you see pus coming out; and when medicine does not help there, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all, and the cure of those is hard work and skill of the experienced doctors on which one must rely.
But in that which is not penetrating and does not become old, healing with iron is expected according to what you recommend, and that which one has tried and proven.
And the patient lies on his back with his knees raised up under him, his hips bent towards his stomach, and then insert a probe of lead or brass into there if it is painful in the anus; and know the place where the probe goes through.
And if he feels that around his neck, then have him lie on his side with his neck bent.
And if you feel the probe already inserted there pushing to penetrate, or else that which one feels between his finger and it swells up or hardens, then know certainly that it is penetrating; and then do not worry about it, for there is no cure for that, as we have said.

Of the cure of wounds and the quality of blistering with medicines.
When there is an abscess on some fleshy part and the space of the abscess is prolonged so that it makes a collection of pus, and then it is broken and opened up, and the pus is discharged, and the wound is left hollow as if it were a vessel, and the membrane which is above it like a thin membrane, and there has not been any subsequent corruption and it does not impress upon the bone or nerve or ligament, it is called a sinus, and is not named fistula.
But if the space is prolonged so that corruption inflicts upon some part, then it is called a fistula and not a sinus.
And the cure of it is that you cut away that membrane entirely as it is turned over, and especially when it is thinned out and made with a dressing, and cauteries so that they do not adhere to the wound by their corruption.
But if you hope that the membrane adheres and that the corruption has not achieved through subsequent consequence, and when there is swelling in the face, then you cure it with blistering.
And it is to be considered a sinus; and if it is great, and the arm which you hold is of fetid odour, then blister that with Egyptian medicine which is of oil and sour wine and rosemary received in equal parts, and let them be boiled in a vessel over the fire until they are reduced to the consistency of honey.
And then take from it what is necessary and dissolve it in water and honey; and blister with that the sinus and tighten up its opening, and leave the medicine in it for about two hours.
And then take that out with caution; and do this for several days until the sinus is cleansed and the swelling reduced.
And sometimes it is blistered with ash water when that medicine is not present to you; and take ashes of wood and put over them water, and then strain those ashes and blister that with the liquid so that you believe that the sinus is now healed.
And if the patient has no tolerance for Egyptian medicine nor ash water, then blister that with water and honey and crushed rosemary which is mixed together, and blister it with wine and honey mixed, because of the property of honey to cleanse and wash away; and wine makes the sinus adhere, especially when there is excess of astringency and dryness.
And when you do this several times and you believe that the sinus is reduced, then blister that with what makes the flesh grow in it.
And take some thin ointment and dissolve it in rose oil and astringent wine, or blister that with some other medicines and ointments which have the properties which I have written about in the treatment of ointments.

Of section for the illness of children.
This illness is named in our land child, and it is pain which comes in some limbs, and then it is transferred from limb to limb.
And you already see that according to what you are told. I was called to a sick woman in a village, and I uncovered her arm and saw a small inflammation in the vein of the arm, and after she was bled for an hour, I saw that inflammation go away just like worms were pushing against it, but gently, not violently, just as if it were living silver when it moved from place to place.
And then she went into pain in that location and felt it in the muscle, and soon she stood there for an hour.
And then she ran through the rest of the body until she reached the other arm.
And it seemed to me that she was turning herself inside out, as I could see; and I was greatly astonished at her transformation and the speed with which she moved from one member to another. And I had seen before in my own experience that pain can be transferred from one member to another. But I have not seen this with my own eyes as it happened to the woman.
And I did not think that this was the case, but rather that it is because the woman was very old, and had dry skin and exposed veins, and in this she manifested to my senses that moving windiness.
And it is necessary for this to not appear in those who are delicate and have moist bodies with hidden veins.
And when you wish to cure her and the patient feels that pain, then it is not for you to appear before him as we have said, but rather stretch out over him gently and slightly and press over that area where that windiness has been cut off, and cauterize the location.
But if you do not see her with your own eyes, then cure her through the expulsion of wind from the body and with that which expels windiness, and those are pills of myrrh and pills of serapis and their like.
Of drawing out arrows.
Arrows are various depending on the species and depending on the locations of the body where they hit, for their variety is that some have great points and small ones and concave ones and blunt ones, and solid ones; and some have three angles and some have tongues and some have stars.
And the signs of the slave are when an arrow hits him and pierces the bone and is lodged in the sphenoid bone which is above the brain, for then occur violent headache, and scotomata, and vertigo, and heat in the eyes, and inflammation and heat of the tongue, and spasm and confusion of the mind and vomiting of bile, and a strong fever is induced and the speech is disturbed and the voice destroyed, and there is moisture coming out of the wound site like white powder, and his eyes are like water.
And therefore if you see these signs, then refrain from curing and drawing out the arrow unless it is outside.
But the signs of the arrow when it hits the heart are that it is near the left mammilla, and you feel that it is stuck in a hard cause, and it is not a hollow cause; and very quickly the movement of the arrow is like the movement of the pulse, and it draws black blood from the wound, and then coldness of the extremities, and shivering, and fainting; and therefore know that death descends upon him from all sides.
And the signs when the arrow has pierced the lungs are effusion of frothy blood from the wound and swelling of the vessels that are near the neck and the colour of the patient changes and he breathes out a great spirit with force and asks for fresh air.
And if the arrow hits the diaphragm which is in the chest, then the pain is near the small ribs and a great wound is made with violent pain and with sighing, and not all the members of the muscles are affected.
And if the arrow hits the stomach then very quickly you will see from the wound some undigested food.
And if the arrow hits the belly and is lodged in it, then you will see some rent in the wound or the fabric or intestine which has been torn, and there is no hope for a cure by drawing out the arrow.
And if the arrow hits the bladder and urine flows out from it, and some cause proceeds from it to the parts outside, then very quickly there is pain over the patient, then know that this is fatal.
But the other members such as the face, the neck and throat, and shoulders, and the back and ribs, and furcula, and hip, and thigh and their like of the members, are saved according to the majority of cases when the arrow does not hit a vein or artery and it is not a venomous arrow.
And I will account for this to you that I have seen of the nature of these arrows so that you may take note of it regarding your own cure.

And the Jew was healed and did not suffer harm to his eye.

Here is a description of forceps used for removing arrows:
The ends of these forceps are shaped like bird beaks and serrated so that they can grip the arrow tightly without slipping.
And from these are made many different kinds of forceps, large, small, and medium-sized, depending on the size of the arrow, its depth, the width of the wound, and the tightness of the flesh around it.

Here is a description of phlebotomy, or the cutting of veins:
The veins from which blood is usually bled are: Those in the head number sixteen, two pulsating veins that are near the ears, two green veins that appear on the temples, two veins in the lacrimal glands of the eyes that are called 'aspicents', one vein at the middle of the forehead, and one vein at the tip of the nose; two veins at the neck; two veins at the upper lip; two veins at the lower lip; and — these are called 'algelic' —; and two veins below the tongue. The veins in the arms that should be bled number five: One of these is the cephalica vein, which resembles a wild leek and is called by the people “the head vein”, it is a superficial and small vein composed of one branch of the basilica and another of the cephalica; and the people call it “the vein of all the body”, the basilica is the vein that lies on the domestic side and is called “citellar” and the commoners call it “vein of the belly”; and the funis of the arm, which lies over the elbow and is where phlebotomy is performed; and salvatella, which is the vein between the ear and the little finger, and has two branches.
There are three veins in the leg: one is below the jaw of the wild side, the second is sophena, and lies below the leg on the domestic side; and the sciatic vein, which lies below the leg on the wild side; and there are other veins in the leg similar to these.

And phlebotomy of the wrist of the right hand is related to diseases of the liver.
And the quality of phlebotomy of it is that you tighten the hand with a ligature or your hand, because you have immersed it in warm water, until the vein is swollen and is demonstrated obviously to the eye; and let the incision be made transversely on it; and if the incision is made anywhere else, then it will not benefit at all.
And take care that you do not cut your hand with the phlebotomy, because underneath there are nerves of the fingers stripped of flesh.
Then immerse the hand in warm water and let the blood run in that, because if you do not immerse it in the warm water, the blood will clot in the orifice of the vein, and its flow is prohibited.
And when the blood is withdrawn according to the amount of need, then let you pause on the vein olive oil and salt, so that it does not clot firmly; and similarly, let you do in every branch tightly.
But the benefit of phlebotomy of that of the left hand is related to diseases of the brain.
And do similarly in the phlebotomy of it as you have done in the other one.
And the benefit of phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is to diseases that are below the body, as well as diseases of the mother, and to retention of menstruation, and to diseases of the knees and ulcers of the legs, and their likes in diseases.
And the quality of phlebotomy of it is that the patient immerses his foot in warm water and rubs on that so that the veins appear; and press on the joint of the foot with a strap; and the location of the vein let it be seen from the toe pushing, and from there it branches into the fascia of the foot many branches.
And therefore phlebotomize the wider ram or below the leg from where the toe comes, because it is better and more safe.
And if phlebotomize that in the fascia of the foot, then you must guard against the nerves that are below that on the fascia of the foot; and let your phlebotomy be made according to the cross, just as if you wanted to make an incision.
And let the phlebotomies be mild.
But if the flow of blood is prohibited, then immerse his foot in warm water and let the blood run in that until it is evacuated.
If the phlebotomist makes a mistake on the first attempt, then repeat with the phlebotomy on a few more occasions, for the site is salty; and do not fear ill will from him, but rather protect yourself from the nerves as we have said.
And similarly, treat the other foot in the same way.
And for the sciatic vein, the site as we have said is below the ankle of the wild part.
And the cause of pain in the hip when phlebotomy is performed is because of clotted blood.
And the quality of the phlebotomy is that the patient enters into a bath, and hastens and tightens his thigh from the side of the buttock up to above the ankle in four places with one long, thin bandage, for it does not appear except through this.
And therefore, when it appears, then you phlebotomize that according to each plan that is possible for you and according to the cross, and it is better; or pierce that through incision or cut that through section, for the site of it is salty.
And it is in most men hidden; and if you pierce that and it does not appear to the senses at all, then phlebotomize a branch of it, and it is that which appears on the back of the foot opposite the little finger and the ring finger.
And beware of the nerves, and let blood flow in the quantity that you want.
Then loosen the tightness and place a bandage over the site of the phlebotomy tightly, for quickly it will be healed.
Of ventilation and of the qualities of using those.
Ventozes are of horn, or of ram, or of bone.
And ventilation is done according to two manners: one of them is with scarification and extraction of blood, and the other is ventilation without scarification.
And this ventilation which is without scarification is according to two manners, for it is either with fire or without fire.
And to the ventozes that are administered with scarification and extraction of blood there are fourteen locations in the body, of which one in which ventozes are placed is the alnocrati, and it is the posterior part of the head; and the middle location of them of two parts of the neck together; and ventozes of the mentum below the mandible further down from the mouth; and ventozes of two spatulas; and ventozes of the aloetaster, and it is above the end of the tail; and ventozes of two focils, and they are of two breasts; and ventozes of two cavilhas; and ventozes of two thighs.
And ventilation does not extract blood except from the small, sparse veins in the flesh, and for this reason it does not possess the virtue of those as cauterization through phlebotomy; nor do I believe that ventilation should be done with fire or without fire in any disease caused by fullness when the whole body is evacuated.
But if necessity prompts ventilation for disease or for continuous, we administer that throughout the entire hour at the beginning of the month and in its end, and in the middle location of it and in any time it is.
And therefore when you see in any cause of those, and particularly if it is in the third or middle of the month, then we command ventilation to be done after two or three hours have passed of the day.
And advantage of ventilation of the nape is that when it conferes to heaviness in the head and to that which is sunken into the eyes; but beware that this be proper evacuation of the whole body.
And this ventilation is in the site of phlebotomy of the cephalic.
And it is not proper to use that which is of complex friction, or in which there is catarrh, for it gives great harm to him.
And for this reason it is not proper that use of that by the elderly, and in heads in which there are cool diseases.
But to him for whom that is continually over him is done forgetfulness; and for this reason you command the ventozador to pause his hand a little with the ventosa at the sites below, fearing the generation of forgetfulness.
But ventilation of alkehel is in the site of phlebotomy of the fusca vein and of the basilica: and for this reason it confers to asthma, and to constriction of throat and to splitting of voice of throat, and to cough, and to fullness.
And it is proper that the ventosa of alkehel be bound a little, for if it is placed below, it generates weakness in the body and in the stomach.
And ventilation of alhagdahan confers to accidental pains in the head and to ophthalmia, and to emigration, and to pre-fever, and to pains in the roots of teeth; and it is in the site of phlebotomy of the basilica.
And you command the ventozador not to press his hand with scarification so that it does not pierce the artery and cause flow of blood.
And ventilation below the mentum confers to alcola in the mouth, and to corruption of the gum, and to appearance similar to them of diseases which are in the mouth, and they stand in the site of phlebotomy of aligeric which is on the two lips.
But the ventilation which is done over the two spatulas confers to caphatan, which is done through fullness of heat.
And the ventilation of the two sites within the breasts confers advantage of phlebotomy of three veins, that is, of the basilica, fusca and cephalic, for it attracts blood from all those veins more delicate than the other veins more coarse within that the attraction reaches the three veins.
And command the ventozador that he does not proceed with scarification, for the site is deprived of flesh, and below it are nerves and arteries.

But ventozes of the thighs be done through mild mildness, for they will draw blood from all the body, and confer to ancient pains of the knees and of the mother, and of the bladder; and they provoke menstruation, and confer to bothor and ademil; and they stand in the site of phlebotomy of two sophenas, and under the armpits, and of two cavilhas.
However they weaken all the body and cause syncope in some men.
And of the ventozes under the armpits, the advantage is similar to the advantage to the ventozes of two thighs.
The quality of the cupping position is that you place the cup at the beginning, let it suck with moderate suction, and do not prolong the position of the cups, but place them lightly, and leave them tightly, so that the humors come to the site evenly. And do not cease to iterate this and continue until you see the site already reddened and swollen and the heat of the blood appearing.
And then you scarify and iterate the suction little by little, and then consider the disposition of the body.

And command the cupper to enlarge the scarification and take a few, and temper the suction with ease.

And if the blood is very foul, then let the third cupping be done so that the residue is achieved.
And finally, when we want to extract blood little by little or a little bit, it is enough for us with one scarification.
And if we want to extract a lot of blood, we scarify with a deep scarification; and if we know that the blood is thick, then let us scarify with a gentle scarification.

And let the cupping be kept away from bathing; while having it administered after bathing for one or two hours, and let him sleep after the cupping.

Thus begins the discussion of the third part: of restoration. This chapter is many times necessary in the art of medicine; and it is a restoration from fracture and from accidental dislocation in the bones.
Oh son, wise one, know that the foolish doctors and the common people and those who have never read the books of the Ancients nor understood a letter from them speak foolishly about this chapter.
And for this reason, this way of the wise in our land is prohibited; and I have not committed it at any time; and I have not practiced that which teaches, unless through length of reading the books of the Ancients and studying on the understanding of those, so that I extracted the science from them.
And I also added experience and study throughout the length of my life.
And I have already described in this chapter everything that my science and experience teach me about this subject; for I have abbreviated it for you and delivered it from the occupation of lengthening; and I have shortened it at the end of the declaration, and illustrated it with many forms of the instruments which are administered in it, as has been done by addition of declaration, as I have done in two preceding chapters.
First chapter.
Summary and collections from being fractured bones, from which their premise is necessary before we begin the remembrance of the members of fractures and of various ones if one by one. Therefore let us begin the summary of this chapter, and the chapters to the understanding of which at the beginning the artisans are forced, and to the truth of those: it is you and those who are adept in this noble art that you learn from others.
And therefore I say that when accidents happen to any fracture or dislocation or twisting or herniation, then let you be concerned at the beginning with phlebotomy or bleeding him, or with some poultice, if no contrary cause prohibits it, as for weakness of strength, or he is an infant, or old in decrepitude, or there is violent heat, or violent rubbing.
And let him be fed on a diet of green vegetables and meat of birds and crabs; and let him abstain from fatty meats and from filling foods that fill the veins with blood, so that you guard against pus in the affected member, and if you do not fear an effusion of matter.
And let him then return to his first regimen with which he was accustomed.
And therefore when the broken bone begins to be restored, then let the patient be fed on nutritive foods of light and coarse and heavy foods in which there is viscosity, as well as on cooked foods made from wheat with water, rice, and peas, and fish, and fresh meat, and groats, and similar to these, and with this regimen coagulation of the fracture is made more slowly and better.
And know that the fractured bones when they are in old people and in the elderly, it is not possible for them to be continued and consolidated according to their nature because of the drying up of the members of them.
Nature however adjusts and connects the broken bone of every part any cause similar to a clot in which there is thickness that adheres and strengthens it together so that it comes to its end of strength and firmness, as well as fire at the beginning, so that no cause prohibits that from its works.
And for this reason it is necessary that you stop the patient's diet, a diet in which there is heaviness and viscosity and coarseness, as we have said.
And know that the species of fractures are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for the fracture of the bone of the leg is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the head; and the fracture of the bone of the foot is diverse from the fracture of the bone of the arm; and similarly all other members are diverse among themselves.
And I win by remembrance of every species of fracture, explained in my distinct chapter from another.
The diversity of fracture within itself. And the species of fractures of the bone are diverse within themselves; for there is made an arcuate fracture another that occurs in them in stars; and some times the fracture is according to the length of the bone: and they are fractures of the satagia and angles and additional ones, as well as when it is prepared for writing, and a fracture is a fissure in the head, and a fracture is a little crack.
And there is a specific pain or torture unique to the restoration of him according to what will come to his memory distinctly in his places.
Here is another sentence: And because of this, a fracture of the bone is known: it is its tortuousness and that eminence and its appearance to the senses, and from it when you compress it with your hand.
And therefore when there is not in the place a tortuousness apparent or from it not made because of your touching when you touch the bone with motion and the patient finds no great pain, then there is no fracture here.
It is possible that there is tortuosity or a small crack, then it does not behoove you to wince when you touch it with your hand and with compression of all; rather pause over it medicines of which come memory fittingly to the place; and then be tight by subtle constriction.
And know that the place when it is arched and not split into two or anything else that does not happen in it satagie, as all is completely split from its comparison, then behoove you to commence from your hour to rectification of it before what happens in it apostema cautery.
And if in it happens apostema cautery, then let it be for several days until the apostema ceases; and then heal that according to any manner that you can of elevation and of healing.
And know that restoration of it and piercing of it is more difficult than of the bone in which happens satagie.
And then strengthen it according to what will come the memory of it.
But if in the bone so satagies, then is necessary extension of that broken member from two parts, if it is the hand or the foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with two cords or with one cord and the hand.
And be your position with which you pause the member place healing according to its natural figure, while the parts so extend from the broken member, then you perforate that returning those adductions with healing and according to any manner that you can in their places.
And guard yourself according to your strength that does not happen over the patient through your operation of pain or harm; and perforate you according to your power that you apply one bone to another companion according to their best forms.
And behoove you in that hour advantageously you touch with your hand that, and if you see here any diverse cause, rectify that and heal that through the quantity of your virtue.
And avoid violent extension and compression, likewise as many foolish doctors who many times cause apostema cautery through their operation, or cause contraction in the member likewise as we have seen that through the operation of them many times.
And the quality of the constriction of the broken member is that which you consider; for you know that the broken members are diversified in their smallness and in their greatness and in the form of them.
And of those which are small likewise as the arm and the finger and the wrist and similar to them, then let the wrapper be of very fine cloth.
But those which are large likewise as the thigh or the leg, then let the wrapping be firm and hard, for the firm binding connects the large member and strengthens it from all parts with equal constriction, so that in it does not enter a crack.

And then pause between the wrappings of wool or cloth with which is healed the tortuosity of the fracture, if in it is tortuosity; and if not, do not pause in it any cause.

Another way of making a plaster that heals fractures and dislocations and twists: Take assafetida, myrrh, acacia, enula camphor, and mace, which is ground from wild peppercorns, all in equal drachms; myrrh and aloe each in five drachms; aloes in twenty drachms; let them all be ground and sieved and mixed with honey water or with white egg yolk if the patient' skin is injured; then use this plaster which has a good composition and quickly restores broken bones, and confers to many people.
Another way of making a plaster that heals fractures and twists: Take mace and ground white alder in ten drachms each; myrrh and aloe in five drachms each; acacia in six drachms, and Armenian bole in twenty.

Method of a plaster that provides relief to injuries and pain: take myrrh, cedar wood, human hair or horse tail, and white poplar bark, and salt, all in equal parts; grind and sift them, then make into a paste and make the plaster.
But the quantity according to the wound that is bound up, and then loosen it, and consider if there is no pain nor swelling, nor is the bone broken and not displaced from its socket, then do not remove it for several days. And if pain or swelling or inflammation occurs in the area, then cut or remove it in that moment and stop using the plaster.
And take a piece of linen and sponge the wound with warm water, and wash the area with mild vinegar until the swelling and pain are relieved; then let the member rest for an hour; and then place clean wool soaked in sweet wine and oil or rose-colored oil over it overnight to prevent an abscess from forming on the wound, and let the inflammation of the member be eased.
And then repeat this gentle compression and small plaster again and not tighten it with your first compression, and use that until the wound is healed.
And if the abscess and heat and pain and inflammation and all accidents are now relieved, and you have stopped using the plaster and compression as you did at first equally, then repeat that in the same way as before.

And then tighten the compression little by little but not as much as your first compression, and adjust the thickness of the padding according to how the wound is preceded in its memory.
But if you see the area of the fracture dried and weakened but not closed, then know that food is prohibited from coming to it; and therefore moisten that with warm water after total washing of the wound; and let it be for three days; and loosen the compression a little, and through this operation feed the member, and it will heal quickly.
But that which the unskilled restorers of the broken member do another washing when it is not healed at the beginning according to how it covers and is healed according to crookedness, is error according to their operation of them, and great fear, and if they would enlarge the ancient wounds in their sockets and were opened by them.
And I have not seen any of them do this, because it is more reasonable that this should not be done.
Chapter two: Of fractures when they occur on the head.
The kinds of fractures are many, and their shapes are diverse, and the causes are distinct, for any fracture is not caused by the impact of a staff, and the impact pierces all the bone into the brain, which is below the skull, as it does in the trunk, and through that this kind of fracture is named ascia; or the staff pierces one part of the bone, and pierces the flesh around it, and the incision does not pass through the bottom of it; and this kind of fracture is named absolute elevation.
And the crack of these two fractures is large or small, and of the fracture that is compression or traction; and the cause of it is impact with a club or stone on stone, and to them alike.
And this fracture is penetrating that it approaches the membrane that is below the bone; or it is in the membrane of the bone, and the crack of these two fractures is large or small.
And of fracture that is worn away in the bone, in the thinness of the hair, and through that it is named this species fracture capillary.

Chapter iii.
Of the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken except into two parts above all, or one of those some few times, because in it there are two bones, because the part below is cartilaginous, and it is not broken, and nothing happens to it except compression and crookedness and sinusitis.
But if one of the two parts of it is broken, then you must put your little finger into his nose, and close that fracture inside with your index finger, and with your thumb push outward, until the nose returns according to its natural figure. And do this by you with facility, and keep yourself that it does not hurt because of your operation on the patient.
And if the fracture is in the highest part of the nose, and the finger does not reach it, then let it be applied with the extremity of the rod in which there is a small thickness.
And if the fracture is in two parts, then you do that.
And cauterize it for restoration in the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, then you must let him rest the cauterized wound for seven days.
And then put into the extremity of the nose a plug of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part, or put two pegs if the fracture is in two parts, and let there be thickness in the peg of as much quantity that fills the hole of the nose.
And some of the ancient restorers said that the pegs be immersed in vinegar, and changed every day, but I have not seen it; they say that the plug be immersed in honeycombed vinegar with powdered mill; and then let the peg stay until the bone is healed and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes it is inserted in the nose, instead of peg, a peacock feather that is wrapped in linen lion cloth; and let there be the fracture of the nose more violent and that the patient not be prohibited from breathing.
And this cause is not necessary for you; and if you want, do it, and if you want, make pegs.
And if another cauterized wound happens, plaster the nose with cerote or with a poultice soaked in wine and rose oil, or with diaquilon.
But if there is no cauterized wound, then plaster that outward with sesame flour and fennel powder cooked in honey; and then place a lion's tongue over it, and do not put the nose to any cause.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or crushed, then split those, and extract them with instruments that are appropriate for them; and then sew the split, and cure that with what encapsulates and consolidates it, and with plasters that are appropriate.
And if a wound happens inside the nose, then cure that with pegs and administer lead tubes until it is completely healed.
Chapter iv.

Chapter 5.

Chapter 12.
Of restoration of a broken arm.
An arm is composed of two bones which are called the two bones of the forearm; one of which is small and is that which the thumb pushes, and the other is large and is that which is placed below the small one in the part further down.
And often only the larger bone is broken, or the smaller one, and often both are broken at once.
And when the smaller bone is broken on top, then its restoration is easier, and its healing is even easier.
And when it is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And even worse than that is when both bones are broken at once.
Therefore if the bone that is broken is the smaller one on top, then it is convenient for the doctor, when restoring it, to make a small extension with ease so that it is soaked.
But if the larger bone is what is broken, then you must make an even more forceful extension.
And if both bones are broken at once, then you must make the extension with the most force.

And then the assistant extends the arm of the part further down with his hand or with a ligament, and another assistant extends it above similarly.
And then the doctor soaks the bone so that he reduces it according to the best figure that he can.
And if there is swelling in the bone, then you pierce through it with your skill to reduce all pressure in its place.

Chapter XVI.
On the restoration of a broken leg.
Know that in the leg there are two bones of which one is thick and is named after the name of the leg; and the other is thin, and is called the tibia.
And it happens to both types of fracture that happen in the leg what happens to the two bones of the arm, and therefore the restoration of it is the same as the restoration of the arm in every way; and the operation of it is one.
And if both bones are broken together, the leg is turned into many pieces.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg is turned to the back.
And if the thicker bone is broken, bend that one downward, and it is of those causes that are not to you known.
And then administer extension and position and strapping of the splints according to that same way.
But however, if the fracture of the leg is loose having many little bones, then the extension should be less and closer, and you should move with your virtue.
And in the leg there is some cause of operation over the arms, and it is when you align the splints and complete all your operation; then you will take weights of wood which is placed beneath the causes which are placed in soles, and they are made equally of white of palm; ligate those of which are small thickness, and not be of the fingers, and let the length of those according to the length of the leg, and of the toe into the most bottom.
And then wrap around each cloth in two wraps according to the length of it; and pause one over the part of the leg on one side and the other on the other side, and let them be from within to the most bottom place of the foot.
And then tie the two splints in three places of two extremes and in the middle place, for with this tightness the leg is prohibited from leaning to the right and left part, and stands in good position.
And then is administered a staff of wood according to the length of the leg; and let it be paused on it the leg so that it conserve that from movement.
And especially you do that in fracture, and when it has been properly healed.
And then consider each day the leg and study with that by last study, and you keep it from pustule and inflammation or other similar causes; and when any cause of those happens, then pause yourself to it with what is necessary for it to be healed.
Chapter XVII.
Of fractures of the talus and bones of the foot and toes.
The talus does not often sustain a fracture; but fractures of the bones of the foot occur frequently; and fractures of the toes are uncommon, and do not occur unless there is some defect in the structure.
And if there is a fracture of the bones of the foot and these bones are displaced, so that they project beyond one another, then let the patient place his foot on the ground prepared as before.
Then let him put his weight upon that foot which is raised by the displaced bones; then let him press and crush and draw these back into their sockets.
And let an ointment be applied to this, and let the swelling below the foot be bound with a bandage which has two small pieces of wood attached to it at one end.
And then strengthen that foot with a splint, because you have bound it up in bandages and other remedies.
After three or four days have passed, then take off the ligature; for you will find the bones displaced, or broken, or dislocated, and the operation in such cases is the same as that already described.
But if some toes are broken, then restore these; draw them together and bind them as before in the restoration of the fingers of the hand. And for the broken toe, apply a splint according to its length; and let it be bound so that there is a little space between each toe; then below the foot, apply this bandage as before, and strengthen the toe with a ligature.
But if there are two or three or more broken bones, then apply to each toe a splint of its own size wrapped in linen; and strengthen the bandage at the lowest part of the foot so that it stands upon one end of the splint, allowing room for the foot to move freely.
And you should not forget that in all cases you should observe the causes which have been stated before concerning the other fractures, and apply remedies according to the case.
Chapter XVIII.
Of fractures of the pubic bone, the shin-bone and the staff of man.
When there is a fracture of the pubic bone, let her lie on her side with a pillow between her legs; then let her lean forward slightly, and let her be supported from behind.
And let the midwife fill up the pubic bone with her finger, and let it be done in the same way as in the case of a woman who has not yet given birth; then let the woman be pressed, and lifted up little by little so that everything may return to its natural position, and the broken bone may come back into its socket.
And let powder be applied to her back; and when she wants to urinate, let the pubic bone be pressed gently so that she may pass water easily; then reduce it and bring it back again according to the method by which you found it at first; then bring it back to its former position.
And do this for seven days or about that time, for then it will be restored.
And if you wish, take a bladder of an ox and strengthen it with a tube of reed; then insert the whole bladder into her pubic bone, and when the bladder is inflated inside her pubic bone, the fracture will return.
And let it be filled up, and let it stay for a few days according to our recommendation, until she is healed.
And when there is a fracture of the shin-bone in man or woman, then administer remedies and punctures as we have recommended for a fracture of the hip-bone.
And that which is straight is not hidden in these fractures, and there is no need of any special study or understanding to read this book; for it explains clearly the remedies for such fractures, and therefore one can understand them well.
But when there is a fracture of the staff in man, take a piece of white poplar-wood and put it in the staff; then apply a bandage of linen over it, and let it be wrapped up and left for three days or about that time, and it will be healed.
Chapter XIX.
Of the restoration of fractured bones when they are swollen.
It is convenient for one who has a fracture and swelling that the bone should be large like the bone of the thigh or arm, or similar to it, so that you may be careful and apply bloodletting as we have stated before.

But if the fractured bone projects beyond the skin which covers it, then beware that you draw it back and bring it together with your hands gently and with little extension.

Chapter .XX.
Of the cure of a knot that occurs in the path of any fracture.
Many times this knot occurs in the path of healing from a fracture, especially near the joints, and it is visible as a shape on the limb; and very often it prohibits the limb from its natural operation.
Therefore, consider if the knot is recent and administer to it medicines that have dried, like aloes, and myrrh, and mirra, and seseli, and similar things; take some or all of them and mix with wine vinegar, or egg white, or vinegar, and apply that on the knot in a piece of cloth, and tie it around tightly.
Let the tightness remain for several days, and then loosen it and repeat this, while the knot is being soaked; or tighten a piece of lead over it inclined downward, because lead has the property of soaking up anything that is inflamed in the limbs.
But if the knot has already turned to stone and is tight, and it necessitates being cut open painfully, then split it from its highest pus-filled part, and cut out the surplus and prominent part, or pierce it with some resoring agent so that it may be soaked; and heal what was healed.
Chapter .XXI.
Of the restoration of a fracture when it is restored and then the limb becomes weak and frail, not according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and then the limb becomes weak and frail, this does not happen for no reason: one cause is excess of inflammation of the ligament and of its binding made not according to how it should be; or due to excess tightness of the ligaments, preventing nourishment from running to the limb; or due to excess of superfluous heat; or due to superfluous movement at an improper time; or due to lack of blood in the patient's body and debility of it.
And the cure for this is to make the patient eat and soften his heart so that blood multiplies in him; and administer a bath; and apply fomentation and lettuce over it.
And then place a poultice on the limb so that the poultice attracts much nourishment to it, or continue fomentation of it with warm water, until the nourishment runs and it returns to its natural shape.
Chapter .XXII.
Of the cure of broken bones when they are restored crookedly and prohibit their operation from what they should do.
When it happens to the limb already restored that there is healing of crookedness and prominence in the broken bone and knot, and it is fed by the form of the limb, but yet the limb is not prohibited from its natural operation, then do not believe the word of one who examines that limb as though it were newly broken.
But many foolish doctors and restorers have done this in our land; and this operation is very blameworthy and presents great danger, the sign of which is deadly.
But if claudication and knot are recent, then let them be fomented with warm water in which soft herbs are boiled, like leaves of poplar tree and roots of it, and mallow, and similar things; or let there be a plaster made with emollient ointment like white lead well made; or let there be a mucilage of root of poplar tree and let it be rubbed with grease of linseed oil, or let there be a plaster made with that; or let them be bound with fat figures and bound with cotton threads, and similar things to those medicines which are named as brothers' consolations.
And sometimes it is dissolved by constant gentle friction that is made with moist hands, and let the limb be moved to all parts at all hours.
But if crookedness has gone before and is made strong and turned to stone and necessitates cure by iron, then let the pus be above dried up and the continuation of the bone be dissolved, and what superfluity of knot from that doctors with subtle tools, and let ease be administered to it with solicitude and study.
Chapter .XXIII.
Of speech in dislocation.
Dislocation is separation of one of the joints of its place, by which it is prohibited from motion, and the figure of the limb is disturbed, and it brings great pains and injuries to the patient.
Therefore when dislocation occurs, then let it be reduced as soon as possible, and do not delay reducing it, because if it is delayed, the place is pustularized and very difficult to reduce the dislocation.
And for that reason, let it not be soaked or stretched in the time of pustulation because many times it brings spasms and pains.
But when that happens, then let you examine the flabosity of the patient, and then let it be left so that pus is discharged from the pustule; and then let the limb be fomented with warm water and oil, and then let it be reduced with ease.
And all parts should be treated with water from which memory will return to its place.
And I have already ordered the chapters on dislocation according to what is preceded in fracture; from the highest part of the body down to the lowest part.
Chapter 24.
On the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are dislocated if it does not bite; and the dislocation of both is done in one of two ways: they are either slightly loosened from their joints and slightly softened, or they are fully dislocated and completed, provided that they are softened at the joint where saliva flows from the patient's mouth and he cannot hold it closed, and his tongue is wrapped in a cloth.
But when the dislocation of it is slight, then in most cases it will return on its own for some slight cause.
But if the dislocation is entire and complete, then it must be reduced quickly without delay at all.
And it is that the minister hold the patient' head, and let the doctor insert his finger of one hand into the root of the dislocation of the jaw inside the patient's mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or that he insert both fingers together, if the dislocation is on two sides, and let the others hands be outside holding him; and command the patient to cease clenching his jaws and release them in any way; and the doctor should grip the jaw and press it in until it returns to its place.
And if the reduction of it is difficult, and especially if both jaws are fully dislocated, then administer evaporation with hot water and oil, so that the reduction of both is made easier and do not delay the reduction of both at all, as we have said.
And when they are returned and bound, and the patient's mouth is closed and not softened, then place over them a poultice of linen with cerot, made of wax and oil heated; and let it be bound easily with soft bandages.
And let the patient sleep on his back, and let his head be between two pillows, so that he does not clench his jaws to the right or left; and do not force him to chew anything for any reason; let his food be soft meat, so that when the pains are lessened and the jaws are numbed, then he may chew whatever he wants.
And let this be administered with ease, and do not work on the opening of the mouth after eating and drinking and speaking, until the jaw is numbed and healed.
And if the reduction of two jaws when they are dislocated at any time is difficult and not returned to their places, then many times there occur fever and continuous sweating, and very soon the belly of the patient is swollen, and very soon he vomits pure phlegm.
And when you see this, then know that it is a loss; and many times he who has this dies within ten days.
Chapter 25.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated on the inside due to its continuation with the shoulder; and sometimes it is dislocated at the outer end, and this is declared by feeling.

But the extremity of that which connects to the head and is continued with it, is not dislocated in any day; then let it be reduced and bound according as we have said and as you are equipped.

And with this method of treatment, the extremity of the muscle is reduced when separated from its place.
Chapter 26.
On the reduction of the dislocation of the muscle.
Know that the muscle is not dislocated except in three ways, of which the first is that it be dislocated at the joint of the shoulder to the lower part.
And the second is that it be dislocated above the muscle, and this is done tightly; and it is not dislocated at the back parts by the shoulder; and it is not dislocated at the front parts by the nerves.
And most often it is dislocated and out from the lower parts behind the shoulder, and most often in those of whom the flesh is thin, it is dislocated easily; but in those in whom there is much flesh, it is forced out with difficulty.
And very often it happens to some men by impact or blow, and the muscle of him is swollen with a hard swelling, and it is bitten by that which has already been dislocated.
Therefore let you do this so that you may be experienced in it, and then proceed with its treatment.

If it is dislocated behind or above the shoulder, and you see a clear touch, and it is not hidden.

Chapter .XXVII.

Chapter .XXVIII.
Of healing of dislocation of malacet, so is the joint that is between the arm and the hand.
